\begin{itemize}

<score>S</score>

General approach: Consider the game as a two-player game with state (S, Q) after each turn, but note that the constraints depend on the number of terms. It might be easier to treat the game in terms of "average" and "average square". Define a_n = S_n / n (the average of x_i), and b_n = Q_n / n (the average of squares). The constraints are: for odd n, a_n ≤ λ; for even n, b_n ≤ 1. However, the constraints are only at the end of each player's moves, not at all times. But we can still consider the state after each full round.

Alternatively, we can consider the following: Alice wants to make the sum of squares exceed n after her move. Since she controls odd indices, she can try to put a large number at some odd step. But the sum constraint limits the total sum. Similarly, Bazza wants to make the sum exceed λ n after his move.

Observation: The game is similar to a "balanced" game where each player has a resource (sum or sum of squares) that they can increase but must keep below a bound. The other player's resource is then affected.

Perhaps we can transform the game into a "potential" game where each player's "potential" is something like S_n^2 - λ^2 n? Not exactly.

Thus, we need to determine for each λ, whether Alice has a strategy to force a win, or Bazza has a strategy to force a win, or both can force a draw (i.e., infinite play). Let's analyze the game more generally. This is reminiscent of a "balanced" game where the constraints are cumulative and each player can control a certain resource. We can treat the game as a kind of "linear" game where the state is (S, Q) and the bound depends on n. However, we can consider that the players can choose numbers to "fill" their constraints, and the game may be analyzed by comparing the "average" of x_i and the "average square" and using the inequality (∑ x_i)^2 ≤ n ∑ x_i^2.

Let's denote after each turn, we have S_n and Q_n. The constraints are:

- For odd n: S_n ≤ λ n.
- For even n: Q_n ≤ n.

We can also think of the "deficit" functions: define D_n = λ n - S_n for odd n, and E_n = n - Q_n for even n. These are nonnegative. The players can choose x_n to increase these deficits or to keep them nonnegative.

We need to find the outcome under optimal play. Since both players can always choose 0, the game can be drawn if both players avoid losing. So the question is: can one player force a win by pushing the opponent's constraint to be violated? This is like a "poison" game where each player can decide to "refill" their own resource or to increase the opponent's resource.

Perhaps we can transform the game into a simpler one by considering "potential" functions. Let's try to derive a necessary condition for Bazza to have a winning strategy. Suppose λ is such that Bazza can force a win. Then there exists a strategy for Bazza to make S_{2k} > λ(2k+1) for some k. Since S_{2k} ≤ √(2k Q_{2k}) ≤ √(2k * 2k) = 2k, we need λ(2k+1) < 2k, which for large k requires λ < 1. So if λ ≥ 1, Bazza cannot win. But we already saw that for λ=1, Alice wins. So if λ ≥ 1, Alice wins. So the interesting range is λ < 1. For λ < 1, Bazza might be able to force a win. But also Alice might be able to force a win for some λ < 1? For λ=0.5, we saw that Bazza can potentially win? Let's analyze more systematically.

We need to find the threshold λ0 such that:

- For λ > λ0, Alice wins.
- For λ < λ0, Bazza wins.
- At λ = λ0, perhaps draw (or maybe one wins? Need to check).

Our earlier analysis for λ=1 showed Alice wins, so λ0 ≤ 1. For λ=0.9, we need to determine if Alice can still win or if Bazza can force a win. Let's try to see if Bazza can have a winning strategy for λ=0.9. He might try to make S_{2k} > λ(2k+1) for some k. Since S_{2k} is at most 2k, we need λ(2k+1) < 2k, i.e., λ < 2k/(2k+1). For λ=0.9, this is true for k large enough because 2k/(2k+1) → 1. For example, k=10 gives 20/21≈0.952>0.9, so possible. But can he actually achieve S_{2k} > 0.9(2k+1) given the sum squares constraint? He can choose his numbers to be large, but he must keep Q_{2k} ≤ 2k. The maximum possible S for a given bound on Q is achieved by making all numbers equal (by Cauchy-Schwarz). But here he controls even indices, and Alice controls odd indices. He can influence the sum by his choices, but Alice can also choose her numbers to limit the sum. For Bazza to win, he must force the sum before Alice's turn to exceed λ times the next odd bound. He can try to build up the sum gradually, but Alice can choose to keep the sum low by choosing small numbers. So we need to analyze the game as a "matching" of two sequences: Alice's numbers (odd) and Bazza's numbers (even). The constraints are:

- After each odd turn, the sum of all numbers so far is ≤ λ n.
- After each even turn, the sum of squares so far is ≤ n.

We can think of the game as each player trying to maximize the "potential" that hurts the opponent. This is similar to a "continuous" game where each player can choose numbers to "refill" a resource.

Perhaps we can find a "strategy" for Alice to win for all λ > 1, and for Bazza to win for all λ < 1, and at λ=1 it's a draw? But we already found that for λ=1, Alice wins. So that's not a draw. Another approach: Think of the game as each player having a "resource" that they can use to "harm" the opponent. At the start, before any moves, S_0=0, Q_0=0, and the "slack" for Alice at turn 1 is λ*1 - 0 = λ. She can choose x_1 up to λ. Similarly, Bazza at turn 2 has slack 2 - Q_1. So the game is essentially about who can force the other's slack to be exhausted.

We can try to analyze the game in terms of "worst-case" for each player. Suppose Alice wants to win. She needs to make Q_{2k-1} > 2k for some k. She can try to do this by setting a large number at some odd step. But she must keep S ≤ λ n. So she needs to have enough "sum" to allow a large square. The maximum square she can achieve for a given sum S is S^2 (by concentrating all mass in one variable). So if she can make S_{2k-1} as large as possible, she can get Q_{2k-1} up to (S_{2k-1})^2. She wants (S_{2k-1})^2 > 2k. So she needs S_{2k-1} > √(2k). But she is limited by S_{2k-1} ≤ λ(2k-1). So if λ(2k-1) > √(2k) for some k, she might be able to achieve that. For large k, λ(2k-1) ~ 2λk, and √(2k) ~ √2 √k. So for large k, 2λk > √2 √k if λ > 0, so eventually holds. So she can always make S large enough? But she also must consider that Bazza's choices may limit S. She can choose her previous odd numbers to be small, but Bazza may choose his numbers to increase S, because he wants to avoid losing? Actually, Bazza wants to avoid being blocked; he might try to keep S low to limit her ability to concentrate mass. Because if S is low, she cannot make a large x_n, so Q cannot become huge. So Bazza will try to keep S low. So the game is about control of the sum.

Perhaps we can think of the game as a "balance" between the two constraints. Since the sum of squares constraint for Bazza is Q_n ≤ n, and the sum constraint for Alice is S_n ≤ λ n, we can consider that the two players are essentially competing to keep their respective constraints satisfied. The game ends when one player's constraint cannot be satisfied even by choosing 0, i.e., the previous sum (or sum of squares) exceeds the bound.

Thus, the game is about who can force the opponent's cumulative sum (or sum of squares) to exceed the bound before the opponent's turn.

We can treat the game as a "two-player" game where each player can decide to "spend" their own resource to increase the opponent's resource. This is reminiscent of a "Chicken" game.

He has more room. Let's see if he can force a win for λ=0.9. We need to analyze the game in general.

We can think of the game as a "continuous" version of the "Miser" game. Perhaps we can find a "potential" function that is monotonic and the game is determined by whether λ is greater than some critical value.

Let's define after each round, we have S_n and Q_n. The constraints are:

- After odd n: S_n ≤ λ n.
- After even n: Q_n ≤ n.

We can also derive that for any n, S_n^2 ≤ n Q_n. So if we define the "deficit" D_n = λ n - S_n for odd n, and E_n = n - Q_n for even n, these are nonnegative. The players can choose x_n to increase these deficits or to decrease them.

Maybe we can transform the game into a "linear" game by considering that the two players are essentially "filling" two sequences: one with sum constraint, the other with sum-of-squares constraint. There is a known result: In the game where Alice chooses x_n with sum ≤ n, and Bob chooses with sum of squares ≤ n, the outcome is that Alice wins if and only if λ > 1? But we saw for λ=1, she wins. So maybe λ > 1/2? Let's test λ=0.6. Could Bazza win? Let's try to see if Bazza can force a win for λ=0.6. He might try to set x_2=1, then Alice can set x_3 up to 1.8-1=0.8? Actually, λ=0.6, so at turn 3, bound = 1.8. So if x_2=1, she can set x_3=0.8, then S_3=1.8, Q_3 = 1 + 0.64 = 1.64. Then Bazza at turn 4: he can set x_4 up to sqrt(4-1.64)=sqrt(2.36)=1.536, then S_4=3.336, Q_4=1.64+2.36=4.0. Then Alice's bound at turn 5 = 3.0. S_4=3.336 >3.0, so she loses. So Bazza wins. But what if Alice chooses a different x_3? She could set x_3=0.5, then S_3=1.5, Q_3=1+0.25=1.25, Bazza's max x_4 = sqrt(4-1.25)=sqrt(2.75)=1.658, then S_4=1.5+1.658=3.158, still >3.0? 3.158 >3.0, yes. So she loses. If she sets x_3=0, then S_3=1, Q_3=1, Bazza's max x_4 = sqrt(3)=1.732, S_4=2.732, still >3.0? 2.732 <3.0, so that would not exceed. So she can choose x_3=0 to avoid losing. Then S_4=2.732, which is less than 3.0. Then game continues. So for λ=0.6, Bazza might not win on his second move if Alice plays optimally. So we need to analyze further. Perhaps Bazza can win by a longer strategy.

Let's attempt to compute the "critical" λ by assuming that the players play optimally to maximize their own potential. This is similar to a "pursuit" game where each player wants to push the other's cumulative sum beyond a bound.

Define after each turn, we have state (S, Q). But the bound depends on n, so we need to keep track of n as well. However, we can consider that the game is "determined" by the "average" of x_i and the "average square". Maybe we can use a "potential" function like φ(n) = S_n^2 - n Q_n? That might be something like the "excess" that could be controlled. Let's compute for the game.

We have the inequality S_n^2 ≤ n Q_n. So φ(n) = S_n^2 - n Q_n ≤ 0. The game constraints are S_n ≤ λ n for odd n, Q_n ≤ n for even n. So for odd n, we have S_n^2 ≤ λ^2 n^2, but Q_n may be larger. So φ(n) could be negative.

Maybe we can consider that if Alice can force φ(2k-1) to be positive? But φ is always ≤0 by Cauchy. Actually, S_n^2 - n Q_n ≤ 0, so φ is non-positive. So that's not helpful.

Another approach: Use a "strategy" where each player chooses numbers to keep the "average" at a certain level. This is reminiscent of the "Moser" game.

Let's try to find a winning strategy for Bazza when λ is small. He might try to set x_2 = sqrt(2) (max) and then at later moves, set x_{2k} = something to make S_{2k} large. But is it possible that for λ = √2/2, Bazza wins? We saw that with optimal play, the game can be drawn (infinite play). Could Bazza have a strategy to force a win? For λ=√2/2, we simulated with x1=0, x3=0, and got that the sum is always just below the bound, so no win. Could he choose a different x2 to eventually win? Now, for λ just above √2/2, say λ=0.8, we saw that with Alice always playing 0, she can survive. So draw. So the threshold for Bazza's win is λ < √2/2. For λ > √2/2, Bazza cannot win. But Alice can win for λ > 2√2/3. So there is a region where neither wins (draw). So the answer is:

- Alice wins if λ ≥ 2√2/3.
- Bazza wins if λ < √2/2.
- Draw if √2/2 ≤ λ < 2√2/3.

But we need to verify the exact threshold for Alice's win. We proved that if λ > 2√2/3, she can win by setting x1=0 and then x3=3λ-x2. Is that the best possible? Could she win for λ = 2√2/3? We argued that she can win by playing x1=0, x3=√2, then x5=4√2/3, causing Bazza to be blocked at turn 6. So yes. So λ = 2√2/3 is a winning value for Alice. So the set where Alice has a winning strategy includes λ ≥ 2√2/3.

Now, we need to prove that for λ < √2/2, Bazza has a winning strategy. Let's construct a strategy for Bazza when λ < √2/2. For such λ, we can set x2 = √2. Then S2 = √2, Q2 = 2. Then Alice at turn 3 can choose any x3 ≤ 3λ - √2. Since λ < √2/2 ≈ 0.7071, we have 3λ - √2 < 2.1213 - 1.414 = 0.7073. So the slack is less than 0.7073. Alice may set x3=0 or some positive. If she sets x3=0, then S3 = √2, Q3 = 2. Then Bazza at turn 4: he can set x4 = √(4 - 2) = √2, S4 = √2 + √2 = 2√2 ≈ 2.828. Now his square budget is Q4 = 4. Then Alice at turn 5: slack = 5λ - 2√2. Since λ < √2/2, we have 5λ < 5√2/2 ≈ 3.5355, and 2√2≈2.828, so slack > 0.7075. She can set x5=0. Then S5 = 2√2. Then Bazza at turn 6: x6 = √(6 - 4) = √2, S6 = 2√2 + √2 = 3√2 ≈ 4.2426. Continue. So after his k-th move, S_{2k} = k√2. Then the win condition is k√2 > λ(2k+1). Since λ < √2/2, we have 2λ < √2. So for sufficiently large k, k√2 > λ(2k+1) because the left side grows like k√2 and the right side grows like 2λk, and since √2 > 2λ, eventually the left side exceeds the right side. More precisely, the inequality k√2 > λ(2k+1) is equivalent to k(√2 - 2λ) > λ. Since √2 - 2λ > 0, there exists k such that this holds. So Bazza can win by playing x2=√2, and then always playing the maximum square (x_{2k}=√(2k - Q_{2k-1})). But we must consider that Alice may not set x3=0; she could set a positive x3. However, if she sets a positive x3, then the sum after Bazza's move at turn 4 is S4 = √2 + x3 + √(2 - x3^2). This is at least √2 + √2 = 2√2 (since the minimum of g(x)=x+√(2-x^2) is √2 at x=0, and for x>0, g(x) > √2). So S4 > 2√2. Then later, the sum will increase even faster, making it even easier for Bazza to win. So this strategy works regardless of Alice's choices. Thus, for λ < √2/2, Bazza has a winning strategy.

Now, for λ between √2/2 and 2√2/3, we need to show that the game is a draw (no winning strategy for either player). That is, both can avoid losing indefinitely. We already have a strategy for Alice: set x1=0, and then at each odd turn, set x_{odd}=0 (i.e., she always plays 0). Then we need to show that Bazza cannot force a win. We already argued that if Alice always plays 0, then after each of Bazza's moves, the sum S_{2k} = √2 + (k-2)√2 = (k-1)√2? Actually, we need to check: At the start, S0=0. Alice sets x1=0, so S1=0, Q1=0. Bazza sets x2=√2, so S2=√2, Q2=2. Then Alice sets x3=0, so S3=√2, Q3=2. Bazza sets x4=√2, so S4=2√2, Q4=4. Then Alice sets x5=0, so S5=2√2, Q5=4. Bazza sets x6=√2, so S6=3√2, Q6=6. So indeed S_{2k} = k√2 for k≥1? Check: For k=1, S2=√2, yes; for k=2, S4=2√2; for k=3, S6=3√2; so S_{2k}=k√2. Then the bound for Alice at her next odd turn (n=2k+1) is λ(2k+1). Since λ > √2/2, we have λ(2k+1) > (√2/2)(2k+1)=k√2 + √2/2. So S_{2k}=k√2 < λ(2k+1) for all k. Therefore, Alice can always move by setting x_{2k+1}=0. So the game goes on forever. So Alice can force a draw. Could Bazza have a winning strategy? He would need to ensure that no matter what Alice does, he can eventually block her. But we have a counter-strategy for Alice (playing 0) that avoids losing. So Bazza cannot have a winning strategy. Therefore, for √2/2 < λ < 2√2/3, the game is a draw (infinite play). At λ = √2/2, the same counter-strategy works: if Alice plays 0, then S_{2k}=k√2, and λ(2k+1)= (√2/2)(2k+1)=k√2+√2/2 > k√2, so she can always move by 0. So draw.

At λ = 2√2/3, we have a winning strategy for Alice, so she wins. For λ > 2√2/3, she also wins by the same strategy (x1=0, then x3=3λ-x2). So the classification is:

- Alice wins if λ ≥ 2√2/3.
- Bazza wins if λ < √2/2.
- Draw if √2/2 ≤ λ < 2√2/3.

Now, we need to check the value λ = 2√2/3 ≈ 0.9428. Is it possible that for λ = 2√2/3, Bazza could have a winning strategy? We already gave a strategy for Alice that wins. So no.

Thus, the answer.

Now, we need to produce a rigorous proof.

Let's structure the proof:

We'll define the game. We'll first handle the case λ ≥ 2√2/3. Show Alice has a winning strategy: She chooses x1=0. Then after Bazza's move at n=2, she chooses x3 = 3λ - x2. Need to show that Q3 = x2^2 + (3λ - x2)^2 > 4. This is true because the minimum of f(x)=x^2+(3λ-x)^2 over x∈[0,√2] is at x=3λ/2, value 9λ^2/2. Since λ ≥ 2√2/3, 9λ^2/2 ≥ 9*(8/9)/2 = 4. So f(x) ≥ 4. Moreover, for λ > 2√2/3, f(x) > 4; for λ = 2√2/3, f(x) = 4 when x=3λ/2 = √2, but if Bazza chooses x2=√2, then Q3 = 2+ (3λ-√2)^2 = 2+ (2√2-√2)^2 = 2+ (√2)^2 = 4, so Q3 = 4 exactly. Then Bazza's next move at n=4: he must choose x4 such that Q4 ≤ 4. But Q3 = 4, so even if he sets x4=0, Q4 = 4, which is allowed. So he can move. But then we need to show that Alice can still win. For λ = 2√2/3, we can give a different strategy: set x1=0, then at turn 3 set x3 = 3λ - x2 = √2 if x2=√2, or if x2 ≠ √2, then Q3 > 4, so Bazza cannot move. So if Bazza chooses a different x2, Alice wins immediately. If he chooses x2=√2, then Q3=4, so he can move at turn 4. But then we need a strategy for Alice to win later. We can use the strategy: after his move at turn 4, she sets x5 = λ*5 - S4. For λ=2√2/3, if he sets x4 = √2 (since Q3=4, he can only set x4=0 to keep Q4≤4? Wait, Q3=4, he must choose x4 such that Q4 = 4 + x4^2 ≤ 4 => x4=0. So he must set x4=0. Then S4 = √2+0+0? Actually, S3 = x2 + x3 = √2+√2 = 2√2. Then x4=0, so S4 = 2√2. Then Alice at turn 5: slack = λ*5 - S4 = (2√2/3)*5 - 2√2 = (10√2/3 - 6√2/3)=4√2/3. She sets x5 = 4√2/3. Then Q5 = Q4 + x5^2 = 4 + (32/9)=68/9 > 6. Then at turn 6, Bazza must have Q6 ≤ 6, but Q5 > 6, so he cannot move. So Alice wins. So for λ=2√2/3, she has a winning strategy (if Bazza chooses x2=√2, she uses this; if he chooses any other, she wins immediately). So Alice wins for λ ≥ 2√2/3.

Now, for λ < √2/2, Bazza has a winning strategy: He chooses x2 = √2. Then after any move by Alice at turn 3, he will choose x4 = √(4 - Q3) (where Q3 = 2 + x3^2). Then S4 = √2 + x3 + √(2 - x3^2). Since the function g(x)=x+√(2-x^2) has minimum √2 at x=0, we have S4 ≥ 2√2. Then he follows the strategy: at each even turn 2k, he sets x_{2k} = √(2k - Q_{2k-1}). Since Q_{2k-1} ≤ 2k-1? Actually, we need to show that this is legal. It is because he always uses the maximum allowed by his square budget. Then after his move, Q_{2k}=2k. We need to show that eventually S_{2k} > λ(2k+1). We have S_{2k} = (k-1)√2 + S4? Let's derive properly.

Suppose at the start of round k (i.e., after Alice's move at turn 2k-1), we have S_{2k-1} = S_{2k-2} + x_{2k-1}. Then Bazza at turn 2k chooses x_{2k} = √(2k - Q_{2k-1}). Then S_{2k} = S_{2k-1} + √(2k - Q_{2k-1}). But we need to track the evolution. However, we can use an invariant: If Alice always sets her numbers to 0, then the sum after Bazza's moves is k√2. But we need to show that even if she does not set 0, he can still win. Actually, we can construct a strategy for Bazza that forces a win regardless of Alice's choices. For λ < √2/2, we can set x2 = √2. Then after Alice's move at turn 3, we have S3 = √2 + x3. Then Bazza at turn 4: he sets x4 = √(4 - Q3). Since Q3 = 2 + x3^2, we have x4 = √(2 - x3^2). Then S4 = √2 + x3 + √(2 - x3^2). Note that the function h(x)=x+√(2-x^2) is at least √2, with equality at x=0, and maximum 2 at x=1. So S4 ≥ 2√2. Now, after turn 4, Q4 = 4. Then Alice at turn 5: she can choose any x5 ≤ 5λ - S4. Since λ < √2/2, we have 5λ < 5√2/2 ≈ 3.5355, and S4 ≥ 2√2 ≈ 2.828, so slack is at least 0.7075. She may choose x5 = 0 or some positive. Then Bazza at turn 6: he can set x6 = √(6 - Q5). But Q5 = Q4 + x5^2 = 4 + x5^2. So x6 = √(2 - x5^2). Then S6 = S5 + √(2 - x5^2). And S5 = S4 + x5. So S6 = S4 + x5 + √(2 - x5^2) = S4 + h(x5). Since h(x5) ≥ √2, we have S6 ≥ S4 + √2. Similarly, after his move at turn 2k, we have S_{2k} = S_{2k-2} + x_{2k-1} + √(2 - x_{2k-1}^2) = S_{2k-2} + h(x_{2k-1}). So S_{2k} = S_2 + Σ_{i=1}^{k-1} h(x_{2i+1})? Actually, S_2 = √2, and for k≥2, S_{2k} = √2 + Σ_{i=1}^{k-1} h(x_{2i+1})? But careful: S_2 is after his first move, and then S_4 = S_2 + h(x_3), S_6 = S_4 + h(x_5), etc. So S_{2k} = √2 + Σ_{j=1}^{k-1} h(x_{2j+1}). Since h(x) ≥ √2 for all x (with equality only at x=0), we have S_{2k} ≥ √2 + (k-1)√2 = k√2. Moreover, we have S_{2k} = k√2 if and only if all x_{odd} = 0 (since h(0)=√2). So S_{2k} is at least k√2. Now, we need to show that for λ < √2/2, there exists k such that S_{2k} > λ(2k+1). Since S_{2k} ≥ k√2, it suffices to show that k√2 > λ(2k+1) for some k. This is equivalent to k(√2 - 2λ) > λ. Since √2 - 2λ > 0, we can choose k > λ/(√2 - 2λ). So there exists a k such that this holds. Therefore, Bazza will win at turn 2k. This argument works regardless of Alice's choices because we only used that S_{2k} ≥ k√2. And note that we used the strategy of always setting x_{2k}=√(2k - Q_{2k-1}) which is legal as long as Q_{2k-1} ≤ 2k. Since Q_{2k-1} = Q_{2k-2} + x_{2k-1}^2, and we ensure that after each of his moves, Q_{2k}=2k, so Q_{2k-1} ≤ 2k-1? Actually, we need to check that Q_{2k-1} is always ≤ 2k. We have Q_{2k-1} = Q_{2k-2} + x_{2k-1}^2. After his move at turn 2k-2, Q_{2k-2}=2k-2. Then x_{2k-1} is chosen by Alice, and she may set it up to something, but Q_{2k-1} could be > 2k? However, we also have that Bazza's constraint is Q_{2k} ≤ 2k. He will set x_{2k} = √(2k - Q_{2k-1}) only if Q_{2k-1} ≤ 2k. If Q_{2k-1} > 2k, then he cannot move (since even x=0 gives Q>2k). But that would mean Alice already won (since she made Q_{odd} > 2k). So if Alice makes Q_{odd} > 2k, she wins. So in a winning strategy for Bazza, we need to ensure that he can always find a legal move. If Alice ever makes Q_{odd} > 2k, then she wins, so Bazza's strategy must prevent that. In our strategy, Bazza chooses x2=√2, then after that, if Alice sets x3 such that Q3 > 4, then she wins. So Bazza must choose his subsequent numbers in such a way that he can always respond. In our analysis, we assumed that after each of his moves, Q_{2k}=2k. Then Alice's choice x_{2k+1} will be such that Q_{2k+1} = 2k + x_{2k+1}^2. This could exceed 2k+2? No, but we need to ensure that at his next turn, Q_{2k+2} ≤ 2k+2, so he must have Q_{2k+1} ≤ 2k+2. Since Q_{2k+1} = 2k + x_{2k+1}^2, this is ≤ 2k+2 if and only if x_{2k+1}^2 ≤ 2. Since Alice's slack is at most λ(2k+1) - S_{2k}, and λ < √2/2, we have λ(2k+1) ≤ (√2/2)(2k+1) = k√2 + √2/2. Also, S_{2k} ≥ k√2, so slack ≤ (k√2+√2/2) - k√2 = √2/2 < 1.414, so x_{2k+1} ≤ √2/2 ≈ 0.707, so x_{2k+1}^2 ≤ 0.5. So Q_{2k+1} ≤ 2k + 0.5 < 2k+2, so Bazza can always move. So the strategy is legal.

Thus, for λ < √2/2, Bazza wins.

Now, for √2/2 ≤ λ < 2√2/3, we need to show that neither player has a winning strategy. We already have a strategy for Alice that forces a draw: she plays x1=0, and then at each odd turn, she plays 0. Then we must show that Bazza cannot force a win. As argued, if Alice plays 0, then after each of Bazza's moves, S_{2k}=k√2. Since λ ≥ √2/2, we have λ(2k+1) ≥ (√2/2)(2k+1)=k√2+√2/2 > k√2. So the slack for Alice at turn 2k+1 is positive, so she can set x_{2k+1}=0. This leads to infinite play. So Alice can avoid losing. Could Bazza have a different strategy that forces a win? He would need to ensure that no matter what Alice does, he can eventually block her. But we have a counter-strategy for Alice (playing 0) that avoids losing, so Bazza cannot have a winning strategy. Therefore, the game is a draw (i.e., both can avoid losing). So the set is draw.

Thus, the answer.

The minimum of f(x)=x^2+(3λ-x)^2 is at x=3λ/2, value 9λ^2/2. Since λ ≥ 2√2/3, 9λ^2/2 ≥ 4. Hence Q3 ≥ 4. If λ > 2√2/3, then Q3 > 4, so Bazza cannot move at n=4 (since Q3>4). If λ = 2√2/3, then Q3 = 4 only if x2 = √2, otherwise Q3 > 4. If x2 ≠ √2, Alice wins immediately. If x2 = √2, then Q3 = 4, and Bazza must set x4=0 to keep Q4 ≤ 4 (since any positive would exceed 4). Then S4 = √2+√2=2√2. Then Alice at turn 5 sets x5 = 5λ - S4 = 5*(2√2/3) - 2√2 = 4√2/3. Then Q5 = 4 + (4√2/3)^2 = 4+32/9 = 68/9 > 6. Then S_{2k} = S_{2k-1} + √(2k - Q_{2k-1}) = S_{2k-2} + x_{2k-1} + √(2k - Q_{2k-1}). But we can use a simpler invariant: S_{2k} = √2 + Σ_{i=1}^{k-1} (x_{2i+1} + √(2 - x_{2i+1}^2)). Since x_{2i+1} + √(2 - x_{2i+1}^2) ≥ √2, we have S_{2k} ≥ k√2. Now, the inequality k√2 > λ(2k+1) is equivalent to k(√2 - 2λ) > λ. Since λ < √2/2, we have √2 - 2λ > 0, so for sufficiently large k, this holds. Thus Bazza will win at turn 2k for some k. Therefore, Bazza has a winning strategy.

Now, write the final answer.</think><solution>
Let $\lambda>0$. The game is played on turns $n=1,2,3,\dots$ with Alice moving on odd turns and Bazza on even turns.  
After Alice’s move at turn $2k-1$ we have $S_{2k-1}=\sum_{i=1}^{2k-1}x_i\le \lambda(2k-1)$; after Bazza’s move at turn $2k$ we have $Q_{2k}=\sum_{i=1}^{2k}x_i^2\le 2k$.  
The player who cannot choose a non‑negative number satisfying the inequality loses; if the game continues forever neither wins.

We determine all $\lambda$ for which Alice has a winning strategy and all for which Bazza has a winning strategy.  
Set $\lambda_0=\frac{\sqrt2}{2}$ and $\lambda_1=\frac{2\sqrt2}{3}$.

---

### 1. Alice wins when $\lambda\ge\lambda_1$

Alice’s strategy:  
- Choose $x_1=0$.  
- After Bazza’s first move (turn $2$) she chooses $x_3=3\lambda-x_2$, where $x_2$ is Bazza’s choice.

Because $x_2^2\le 2$ (since $Q_2\le2$), we have $x_2\in[0,\sqrt2]$.  
Then $Q_3=x_2^2+(3\lambda-x_2)^2$.  The function $f(x)=x^2+(3\lambda-x)^2$ on $[0,\sqrt2]$ attains its minimum at $x=3\lambda/2$; the minimum value is $\frac{9\lambda^2}{2}$.  
For $\lambda\ge\lambda_1=\frac{2\sqrt2}{3}$ we have $\frac{9\lambda^2}{2}\ge4$, hence $Q_3\ge4$.  

*If $\lambda>\lambda_1$*, then $Q_3>4$ for every $x_2\in[0,\sqrt2]$ because the minimum is strictly larger than $4$ when $\lambda>\lambda_1$ and $x_2\neq3\lambda/2$; even if $x_2=3\lambda/2$ (which is $\le\sqrt2$), we have $Q_3=\frac{9\lambda^2}{2}>4$.  
Thus $Q_3>4$, and at Bazza’s next turn (turn $4$) he would need $Q_4\le4$.  But $Q_3>4$, so even with $x_4=0$ we would have $Q_4=Q_3>4$ – a violation of his constraint.  Hence he cannot move, Alice wins.

*If $\lambda=\lambda_1$*, then $Q_3=4$ only when $x_2=3\lambda/2=\sqrt2$; otherwise $Q_3>4$ and Alice wins immediately.  If Bazza chooses $x_2=\sqrt2$, then $Q_3=4$ and Bazza must set $x_4=0$ to keep $Q_4\le4$ (any positive $x_4$ would make $Q_4>4$).  Then $S_4=\sqrt2+0+\sqrt2=2\sqrt2$.  
Alice’s turn at $n=5$: she may set $x_5=5\lambda-S_4=5\cdot\frac{2\sqrt2}{3}-2\sqrt2=\frac{4\sqrt2}{3}$.  
Then $Q_5=4+\bigl(\frac{4\sqrt2}{3}\bigr)^2=4+\frac{32}{9}=\frac{68}{9}>6$.  
At Bazza’s turn $n=6$ he would need $Q_6\le6$, but $Q_5>6$ already, so he cannot move.  Alice wins.

Thus for every $\lambda\ge\lambda_1$ Alice has a winning strategy.

---

### 2. Bazza wins when $\lambda<\lambda_0$

Bazza’s strategy:  
- At turn $2$ choose $x_2=\sqrt2$ (allowed because $\sqrt2^2=2\le2$).  
- After each of Alice’s moves he chooses the maximum possible number consistent with his square budget: at turn $2k$ he sets $x_{2k}=\sqrt{2k-Q_{2k-1}}$.  (If $Q_{2k-1}>2k$ then Alice already won; we shall see that never happens.)

We analyse the evolution.  After his first move $S_2=\sqrt2$, $Q_2=2$.  Let $x_3$ be Alice’s choice at turn $3$; she must satisfy $x_3\le3\lambda-\sqrt2$.  Since $\lambda<\lambda_0=\frac{\sqrt2}{2}$, we have $3\lambda-\sqrt2<\frac{3\sqrt2}{2}-\sqrt2=\frac{\sqrt2}{2}$, so $x_3\le\frac{\sqrt2}{2}$.  Hence $x_3^2\le\frac12$.

Bazza then at turn $4$ chooses $x_4=\sqrt{4-Q_3}$ with $Q_3=2+x_3^2$, i.e. $x_4=\sqrt{2-x_3^2}$.  Then $S_4=\sqrt2+x_3+\sqrt{2-x_3^2}$ and $Q_4=4$.

We prove by induction that after his $k$‑th move ($k\ge1$) we have  
$S_{2k}\ge k\sqrt2$ and $Q_{2k}=2k$, and that Alice’s choices never cause $Q_{\text{odd}}>2k$ before her move.  The base $k=1$ is true: $S_2=\sqrt2$, $Q_2=2$.  
Assume the statement holds up to $k-1$ ($k\ge2$).  At the start of turn $2k-1$ we have $Q_{2k-2}=2k-2$ and $S_{2k-2}\ge(k-1)\sqrt2$.  Alice chooses $x_{2k-1}$ with $0\le x_{2k-1}\le \lambda(2k-1)-S_{2k-2}$.  Because $\lambda<\lambda_0$, we have $\lambda(2k-1)\le\lambda_0(2k-1)=\frac{\sqrt2}{2}(2k-1)=k\sqrt2-\frac{\sqrt2}{2}$.  Together with $S_{2k-2}\ge(k-1)\sqrt2$ we obtain  
$x_{2k-1}\le \bigl(k\sqrt2-\frac{\sqrt2}{2}\bigr)-(k-1)\sqrt2=\frac{\sqrt2}{2}$.  
Hence $x_{2k-1}^2\le\frac12$, so $Q_{2k-1}=2k-2+x_{2k-1}^2\le2k-2+\frac12=2k-1.5<2k$.  Therefore Bazza can legally set $x_{2k}=\sqrt{2k-Q_{2k-1}}$.  Then $Q_{2k}=2k$ and  
$S_{2k}=S_{2k-1}+x_{2k}=S_{2k-2}+x_{2k-1}+\sqrt{2k-Q_{2k-1}}$.  
Since $x_{2k-1}+\sqrt{2k-Q_{2k-1}}\ge \sqrt{2}$ (the function $g(x)=x+\sqrt{2-x^2}$ has minimum $\sqrt2$ on $[0,\sqrt2]$), we obtain  
$S_{2k}\ge S_{2k-2}+\sqrt2\ge (k-1)\sqrt2+\sqrt2=k\sqrt2$.

Now consider the inequality $S_{2k}>\lambda(2k+1)$.  Because $S_{2k}\ge k\sqrt2$, it suffices to show that $k\sqrt2>\lambda(2k+1)$ for some $k$.  This is equivalent to $k(\sqrt2-2\lambda)>\lambda$.  Since $\lambda<\lambda_0$, we have $\sqrt2-2\lambda>0$, so we can choose $k$ large enough, e.g. $k>\frac{\lambda}{\sqrt2-2\lambda}$.  For that $k$ we have $S_{2k}>\lambda(2k+1)$.  Thus at the start of Alice’s next turn (turn $2k+1$) the sum $S_{2k}$ already exceeds the bound $\lambda(2k+1)$ – she cannot move (even with $x_{2k+1}=0$), so Bazza wins.

Hence for every $\lambda<\lambda_0$ Bazza has a winning strategy.

---

### 3. Draw when $\lambda_0\le\lambda<\lambda_1$

Alice can force a draw by playing $x_1=0$ and then always $x_{2k-1}=0$ for $k\ge2$.  
After each of Bazza’s moves we have $S_{2k}=k\sqrt2$ (since $x_1=0$, $x_2=\sqrt2$, $x_3=0$, $x_4=\sqrt2$, …).  Because $\lambda\ge\lambda_0$, we have  
$\lambda(2k+1)\ge\frac{\sqrt2}{2}(2k+1)=k\sqrt2+\frac{\sqrt2}{2}>k\sqrt2=S_{2k}$.  
Hence the slack for Alice at her turn $2k+1$ is positive, so she can choose $x_{2k+1}=0$ and stay within her sum constraint.  The game thus continues forever, Alice never loses.  Consequently Bazza cannot have a winning strategy (since Alice has a counter‑strategy that avoids losing).  

Therefore for $\lambda_0\le\lambda<\lambda_1$ the game is a draw – neither player has a winning strategy.

---

### Conclusion

- Alice has a winning strategy if and only if $\lambda\ge\frac{2\sqrt2}{3}$.  
- Bazza has a winning strategy if and only if $\lambda<\frac{\sqrt2}{2}$.  
- For $\frac{\sqrt2}{2}\le\lambda<\frac{2\sqrt2}{3}$ the game is a draw (no winning strategy for either player).

Thus the required values of $\lambda$ are:
\[
\boxed{\lambda\ge\frac{2\sqrt2}{3}\ \text{(Alice wins)},\qquad \lambda<\frac{\sqrt2}{2}\ \text{(Bazza wins)},\qquad \frac{\sqrt2}{2}\le\lambda<\frac{2\sqrt2}{3}\ \text{(draw)}.}
\]
</solution>

The case $\lambda=\sqrt2/2$ is handled as a draw because Alice can survive by playing zero; the case $\lambda=2\sqrt2/3$ is covered by Alice’s winning strategy (she wins even when the “slack” strategy gives equality, by a longer sequence).
